reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Simple model that performs min reduction over a specific dimension.
    """
    def __init__(self, dim: int):
        """
        Initializes the model with the dimension to reduce over.

        Args:
            dim (int): The dimension to reduce over.
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.dim = dim

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Applies min reduction over the specified dimension to the input tensor.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor.

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor after min reduction over the specified dimension.
        """
        return torch.min(x, dim=self.dim)[0]

batch_size = 128
dim1 = 4096
dim2 = 4095

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, dim1, dim2)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [1] # Example, change to desired dimension

The input is `[128, 4096, 4095]` and the reduction dimension is `1`. So we are reducing over the middle dimension (4096) of a tensor of shape (128, 4096, 4095). The output shape will be (128, 4095). This is a relatively large reduction. PyTorch likely uses optimized CUB or TensorIterator reductions, which should be quite fast. However, perhaps our custom kernel can squeeze some performance by: 1. Fusing operations if possible (none here). 2. Optimizing thread/block layout for the specific dimensions. 3. Using warp shuffle for in-register reductions. 4. Optimizing memory access patterns. Given the shape (128, 4096, 4095) reducing over dim 1 (size 4096): - We can view the input as (128 * 4095, 4096) rows each of length 4096. - There are 128*4095 ≈ 524k rows. Each row is 4096 elements. - This is a significant amount of parallelism (524k rows). - Each row is 4096 * 4 bytes = 16KB. Total input size = 128 * 4096 * 4095 * 4 ≈ 8.5 GB. That's huge! Wait: 128 * 4096 * 4095 = 2,147,450,880 elements. At 4 bytes per element, that's ~8.59 GB. That is large. The batch size and dimensions are big. Wait, does it fit in GPU memory? Let's check. Typical GPU memory is 16GB or more. So yes, it fits. But 8.5 GB tensor. Reducing over dim 1 (4096) to produce 128*4095 = 524,160 elements output. Total memory read is 8.5 GB. Memory bandwidth will be the bottleneck. The reference kernel for `torch.min` might not be fully memory bandwidth bound if it's not perfectly coalesced or if it does multiple passes. For a custom kernel, we want to read each row once and compute the min. Since each row is 4096 floats. If we assign one thread per row or multiple threads per row, we need to be careful. One approach: - Each block handles one or more rows. - Within a block, use a tree reduction or warp shuffle to find the min of a row. - Since there are 524k rows, we can launch 524k blocks, each with say 256 threads. Each block reduces one row. - The row length is 4096. 256 threads can read 4096 elements in 16 iterations (256*16 = 4096). - Each thread reads 16 elements, computes a local min. - Then do a block-level reduction (shared memory or warp shuffle). - Each block writes one output. Simpler kernel: ```cpp __global__ void min_reduce_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int batch, int dim_reduce, int dim_other, int stride_batch, int stride_reduce, int stride_other) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * dim_other; if (idx >= total) return; int b = idx / dim_other; int o = idx % dim_other; float min_val = INFINITY; const float* ptr = input + b * stride_batch + o * stride_other; for (int r = 0; r < dim_reduce; ++r) { float val = ptr[r * stride_reduce]; if (val < min_val) min_val = val; } output[idx] = min_val; } ``` Wait, but we need to be careful: if dim_reduce is large (4096), and stride_reduce is large (4095), the loop has fixed stride. The compiler can unroll if we add `#pragma unroll`. But 4096 is too big to fully unroll. We can do `#pragma unroll 4` or something. But the memory coalescing for this kernel: - Consecutive `idx` values correspond to consecutive `o` (for same b) because `idx = b*dim_other + o`. - For fixed `r`, consecutive threads access `ptr[r*stride_reduce]` where `ptr` differs by `stride_other`. - Since `stride_other = 1` (the inner dimension), these are contiguous. So coalescing is perfect. - Great. But there is one subtlety: the total number of threads `batch * dim_other`. For contiguous tensor with shape (B, R, C), reducing over dim=1: - `batch = B = 128` - `dim_reduce = R = 4096` - `dim_other = C = 4095` - `stride_batch = R*C` - `stride_reduce = C` - `stride_other = 1` So the kernel is correct for this case. For dim=2 (last dim, contiguous): ```cuda template <int BLOCK_WARPS> __global__ void min_reduce_dim2(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { // Each warp handles one (b, d1) int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int num_warps = BLOCK_WARPS; int global_warp_id = (blockIdx.x * num_warps + warp_id); int total_outputs = B * D1; if (global_warp_id >= total_outputs) return; int b = global_warp_id / D1; int d1 = global_warp_id % D1; const float* ptr = in + b * D1 * D2 + d1 * D2; float min_val = FLT_MAX; // Each thread handles D2/32 elements, strided by 32 // Process with unrolling for vectorization int base = lane; #pragma unroll 8 for (int i = base; i < D2; i += 32) { float v = ptr[i]; min_val = fminf(min_val, v); } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, min_val, offset); min_val = fminf(min_val, other); } if (lane == 0) { out[global_warp_id] = min_val; } } ``` For dim=1 (strided by D2): ```cuda template <int WARPS_PER_BLOCK> __global__ void min_reduce_dim1(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { // Each warp handles WARP_SIZE consecutive d2 values // For B=128, D2=4095, total "output groups" = B * ceil(D2 / 32) = 128 * 128 = 16384 if D2=4096, actually 4095/32=128 int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; } } ``` Wait, for dim=1, each thread in the warp handles one d2, and they load x[b, d1, my_d2] for varying d1. For dim=1 (reducing over the middle dimension of a 3D tensor), there are two classic approaches: 1. Read strided memory (bad coalescing) 2. Process in tiles: read a block of [tile_d1, tile_d2] into shared memory in a transposed/coalesced way, then reduce. For approach 2: we load a tile like [reduction_chunk, output_chunk] into shared memory. For dim=1, the input tile has elements [d1_start:d1_start+chunk_d1, d2_start:d2_start+chunk_d2]. When loading, we can have threads read contiguous memory along d2. That's 256 contiguous floats, perfect coalescing! Then advance to d1+1, load next slice. After loading 16 slices, shared memory has the full tile. Then reduce in shared memory over d1. This tile approach for dim=1: - Block size = 256 threads - Tile size = 16 x 256 (d1 x d2) - For each b: loop over d2 in chunks of 256, and d1 in chunks of 16. - Load tile into shared memory: for each d1 in chunk, all threads load one float each (contiguous along d2). 16 such loads. - After tile loaded, each thread reduces over d1 in its column: find min of shared_mem[0..15][threadIdx.x] - Write result to output. Wait, after loading a tile of 32 elements along D2, we reduce it to a running min. Then we don't need to keep the shm for the next tile, so we can overwrite. But writing the running min requires storing 128 min values. Each warp handles 16 outputs. If only lane 0 writes, that's fine. Or we can have each thread maintain running mins for the outputs it handles. But since warp shuffle gives the min to all threads, we need all threads in the warp to track it, or just use a register. Actually, with warp shuffle, we can do: ```cuda float val = shm[o*32 + lane]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); val = fminf(val, other); } float min_in_tile = val; // now all threads have the same min for this output running_min = fminf(running_min, min_in_tile); ``` But `val` after shuffle is the same in all threads only if we use `offset` correctly and the tree is right. `__shfl_down_sync` shifts values down, so after full reduction, lane 0 has the min. Other lanes don't have the min unless we broadcast it. We can either: 1. Only lane 0 uses it: `if (lane == 0) running_min = fminf(running_min, val);` and then at the end, lane 0 writes to output. 2. Broadcast: `val = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, val, 0);` so all threads have it. Then all can update running_min. Let's use approach 1 to save shuffles. Each warp maintains 16 running_mins, but only lane 0 needs them. Lane 0 can maintain all 16 in an array. ```cuda // In warp w (threads w*32 to w*32+31) if (lane == 0) { float running_mins[16]; for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) running_mins[i] = FLT_MAX; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) { // ... load tile into shm ... __syncthreads(); for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) { float val = shm[(w*16 + o)*32 + lane]; // but lane == 0, so this is shm[...] // No, we need all threads in warp to read for shuffle } } } ``` Actually we need all threads to participate in warp shuffle, so even though only lane 0 keeps the final result, all threads must do the loads and shuffles. ```cuda float running_min[16]; // each thread in warp has this? No, that's 16 registers per thread. ``` Better: have each warp process one output at a time, and lane 0 maintains a running_min register. All other threads just participate in shuffle and throw away results. ```cuda float my_min = FLT_MAX; // only used by lane 0, but all threads have it (wasted) for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) { float val = shm[(warp_output_start + o) * 32 + lane]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); val = fminf(val, other); } if (lane == 0) { my_min = fminf(my_min, val); } } ``` This means each warp does 16 outputs sequentially. For each output, 32 threads read shm and do warp shuffle. Then lane 0 updates. Total shuffles per output = 5 (16,8,4,2,1) = 5 instructions. 16 outputs = 80 shuffles per warp. 128 tiles * 80 = 10,240 shuffles per warp. That's fine. Let me try a simple but vectorized approach. For dim=2 (contiguous reduction over 4095 elements): ```cuda __global__ void min_reduce_dim2(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { int b = blockIdx.y; int d1 = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (d1 >= D1) return; const float* row = in + b * D1 * D2 + d1 * D2; float min_val = FLT_MAX; // Vectorized loop int i = 0; for (; i + 3 < D2; i += 4) { float4 v = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(row + i); min_val = fminf(min_val, v.x); min_val = fminf(min_val, v.y); min_val = fminf(min_val, v.z); min_val = fminf(min_val, v.w); } for (; i < D2; i++) { min_val = fminf(min_val, row[i]); } out[b * D1 + d1] = min_val; } ``` Wait, this uses one thread per output. Block size 256, 128 blocks per b, total blocks = 128 * 16 = 2048. Threads = 2048 * 256 = 524,288. Blocks per SM = 15.5. At 256 threads, max 8 concurrent. Waves = 1.94. But memory locality is bad because adjacent threads read adjacent rows (16KB apart) simultaneously. Each thread does vectorized loads from its row. Actually, with float4 loads, each thread reads 16 bytes at a time. Adjacent threads (lane 0 and 1) read row 0 and row 1. Row 0 at offset 0, row 1 at offset 4095*4 = 16380 bytes. These are in different cache lines. But if the memory controller can issue many requests in parallel, it might still be okay. Wait, adjacent threads in the same warp and block process the same b but adjacent d1. Their rows are adjacent in memory! x[b, d1, :] and x[b, d1+1, :] are adjacent. So as thread 0 reads row 0 offset 0..15, thread 1 reads row 1 offset 0..15, etc. These requests are close together! Memory controller can group them. In fact, all threads in a block reading the first float4 of their rows means requests for offsets 0, 16380, 32760, ... for 256 threads. These are spaced 16380 bytes apart = about 128 cache lines apart. A warp of 32 threads requests 32 cache lines simultaneously. That's 32 * 128 = 4096 bytes per cache line? No, 32 cache lines of 128 bytes each = 4096 bytes total. But they're scattered over a 512KB range. Not good for L2 locality. However, the memory controller on H100 supports many outstanding requests and can handle scattered loads. The main question is bandwidth. For 1D block, thread 0..255 read rows 0..255. Next block reads rows 256..511. These are in adjacent memory. So blocks process adjacent memory regions. L2 might prefetch blocks. But each thread reads 4095*4 = 16380 bytes. With 256 threads, that's 4MB per block. 2048 blocks total. Actually, the key issue is coalescing within a warp. In this kernel, threads in a warp do NOT coalesce! Each thread reads from its own address. So a single warp instruction generates 32 separate memory requests (or 8 memory transactions if the cache line is 128 bytes and addresses within a cache line are grouped). Wait, thread i reads row i at offset j. Address = base + i*D2*4 + j. For j=0, addresses are base, base+16380, base+32760, etc. The difference between consecutive addresses is 16380 bytes. 16380 / 128 = 128 cache lines apart. So each thread's request goes to a different cache line. A warp load instruction results in 32 separate 128-byte transactions (or more precisely, 32 separate requests). This is terrible for memory throughput! The memory subsystem can issue maybe 1-2 requests per cycle per SM. With 64 warps per SM, if each warp generates 32 requests for one load, but only executes one load instruction every many cycles due to memory latency... it's complicated, but scattered loads definitely hurt bandwidth. So the 1 thread per output approach for contiguous reduction is bad. We need cooperative loading. For contiguous reduction, the standard high-performance pattern is to have a warp or block cooperatively load from one output. This ensures coalescing. My tile approach with cooperative loading is the right way. Let me implement it more carefully. For dim=2 tile kernel: - Grid: (D1/128, B) blocks. Each block processes one b and 128 d1 values. - Block: 256 threads (8 warps). - Shared memory: 128 x 32 floats = 16KB. - Tile over D2 with size 32. num_tiles = ceil(D2/32) = 128. Algorithm: ```cuda __global__ void min_reduce_dim2(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { int b = blockIdx.y; int d1_base = blockIdx.x * 128; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; // Shared memory: 128 * 32 floats __shared__ float shm[128 * 32]; float running_min[16]; // Only lane 0 uses, but all threads allocate if (lane == 0) { for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) running_min[i] = FLT_MAX; } int num_full_tiles = D2 / 32; // 127 for D2=4095? 4095/32 = 127 remainder 31 // Actually 4095 = 128*32 - 1, so 127 full tiles of 32 and one partial of 31. const float* in_base = in + b * D1 * D2 + d1_base * D2; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_full_tiles; tile++) { int d2_offset = tile * 32; // Load 128*32 elements into shared memory // Each thread loads 16 elements // Map: thread t loads slice s = t / 256? No, we have 256 threads, 4096 elements. // Each thread does 16 loads. #pragma unroll for (int s = 0; s < 16; s++) { int idx = s * 256 + tid; int o = idx >> 5; // output index in block (0..127) int e = idx & 31; // element in tile (0..31) int d1 = d1_base + o; if (d1 < D1) { shm[o * 32 + e] = in_base[o * D2 + d2_offset + e]; } } __syncthreads(); // Each warp handles 16 outputs int warp_output_start = warp_id * 16; for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) { int out_idx = warp_output_start + o; if (d1_base + out_idx < D1) { float val = shm[out_idx * 32 + lane]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); val = fminf(val, other); } if (lane == 0) { running_min[o] = fminf(running_min[o], val); } } } __syncthreads(); } // Handle partial tile int d2_offset = num_full_tiles * 32; int remaining = D2 - d2_offset; if (remaining > 0) { // Load partial tile, set out-of-bounds to FLT_MAX #pragma unroll for (int s = 0; s < 16; s++) { int idx = s * 256 + tid; int o = idx >> 5; int e = idx & 31; int d1 = d1_base + o; if (d1 < D1 && e < remaining) { shm[o * 32 + e] = in_base[o * D2 + d2_offset + e]; } else { shm[o * 32 + e] = FLT_MAX; } } __syncthreads(); int warp_output_start = warp_id * 16; for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) { int out_idx = warp_output_start + o; if (d1_base + out_idx < D1) { float val = shm[out_idx * 32 + lane]; if (lane >= remaining) val = FLT_MAX; // only needed if remaining < 32 #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); val = fminf(val, other); } if (lane == 0) { running_min[o] = fminf(running_min[o], val); } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output int warp_output_start = warp_id * 16; if (lane == 0) { for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) { int d1 = d1_base + warp_output_start + o; if (d1 < D1) { out[b * D1 + d1] = running_min[o]; } } } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with `running_min` being an array of 16 floats per thread. Let me now write the actual code: ```python kernel_code = r''' import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <float.h> // Kernel for reducing over dimension 2 (last dim, contiguous) // Grid: (ceil(D1/128), B) // Block: 256 threads __global__ void min_reduce_dim2(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { int b = blockIdx.y; Also, by using multiple accumulation registers (min_val[0..3]), we can hide the fminf latency since each iteration's fminf can be independent. Let me update the kernel with this optimization. I'll use 8-way unrolling to better hide latency. Actually, I realize that with this approach, the shared memory is not even needed! The kernel becomes much simpler. Also, for D1=4096, which is a power of 2, the 4-way unrolling fits perfectly. Let me also think about vectorized loads. If each thread loads float4, that would be even more efficient. But float4 requires 16-byte alignment. The address `b*D1*D2 + d1*D2 + d2` depends on d2. For d2 to be aligned to 16 bytes, d2 must be a multiple of 4. Since each block handles d2 from d2_base to d2_base+255, and d2_base is a multiple of 256 (which is a multiple of 4), the first address for each thread in a block: - thread 0: d2 = d2_base (multiple of 256, so aligned to 16) - thread 1: d2 = d2_base + 1 (not aligned to 16) - So float4 loads are NOT aligned for most threads. I could have each thread process 4 d2 values (using a block size of 64 instead of 256, handling 4x more d2 per thread). But this might complicate things. Actually, a simpler approach: since each warp reads 32 contiguous d2 values, and the warp starts at a multiple of 32 (if block size is 256 = 8*32, and d2_base is a multiple of 256), then the warp's access starts at an address that's a multiple of 128 bytes (32*4 = 128). This is perfectly aligned for 128-bit (16-byte) reads. So I could use float4 loads within a warp! If each thread loads 1 float, and adjacent threads load adjacent floats, a warp of 32 threads loads 128 bytes = 8 float4 values. But each thread still just loads 1 float. Alternatively, each thread could load a float4, processing 4 d2 values. Then the block size could be 64 threads handling 256 d2 values. But then the warps would handle d2 in groups of 64*4=256, which is fine. Let me stick with the scalar unrolled version for simplicity. It should already be quite good. Let me design the optimized kernel: ```cuda __global__ void min_reduce_dim1_opt(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int D1, int D2) { int b = blockIdx.y; int d2 = blockIdx.x * 256 + threadIdx.x; if (d2 >= D2) return; float m0 = FLT_MAX, m1 = FLT_MAX, m2 = FLT_MAX, m3 = FLT_MAX; float m4 = FLT_MAX, m5 = FLT_MAX, m6 = FLT_MAX, m7 = FLT_MAX; const float* base = in + (int64_t)b * D1 * D2 + d2; for (int d1 = 0; d1 < D1; d1 += 8) { m0 = fminf(m0, base[0]); m1 = fminf(m1, base[D2]); m2 = fminf(m2, base[2*D2]); m3 = fminf(m3, base[3*D2]); m4 = fminf(m4, base[4*D2]); m5 = fminf(m5, base[5*D2]); m6 = fminf(m6, base[6*D2]); m7 = fminf(m7, base[7*D2]); base += 8 * D2; } float m01 = fminf(m0, m1); float m23 = fminf(m2, m3); float m45 = fminf(m4, m5); float m67 = fminf(m6, m7); float m0123 = fminf(m01, m23); float m4567 = fminf(m45, m67); out[(int64_t)b * D2 + d2] = fminf(m0123, m4567); } ``` Wait, this assumes D1 is a multiple of 8. For D1=4096, it is. But for generality, I should handle remainders. Also, I realize that the loop variable `base` increments by 8*D2 each iteration. With D1=4096, there are 512 iterations. Let me also make sure the compiler generates good code. We want: 1. Loads to be pipelined 2. The 8 fminf operations to be independent (they are, since they use different accumulators) 3. No unnecessary spills to local memory 8 float accumulators per thread = 32 bytes, which easily fits in registers. This should give much better instruction-level parallelism (ILP) than the original loop with a single accumulator. Let me update the kernel accordingly. I'll keep the dim2 and dim0 kernels as they are (though for the specific test, only dim1 matters), and optimize dim1. Actually, I should also handle non-multiple-of-8 D1 values. I'll add a remainder loop. Also, let me make the code cleaner by using a template approach or just handling the general case. Let me rewrite the entire kernel with this optimization.

The access pattern when going over D1 is strided by 4095. Wait! The stride is D2=4095. So adjacent elements in D1 are adjacent in memory... no wait. Layout is [B, D1, D2] row-major. - Element [b, d1, d2] is at offset b * D1 * D2 + d1 * D2 + d2. - For fixed b and d2, as d1 increases, stride between elements is D2 = 4095. So when reducing over dim1, we access elements with stride 4095. This is a large stride but still somewhat coalesced if we handle it right. My current dim1 kernel: - Each thread handles one d2 value. - It unrolls by 8 to process 8 d1 values at a time. - Access pattern: for thread t, it accesses elements at offsets: b*D1*D2 + d2 + 0*D2, b*D1*D2 + d2 + 1*D2, ..., b*D1*D2 + d2 + 7*D2 - Since d2 differs by 1 between adjacent threads, the addresses are: (base + d2), (base + d2 + D2), ... - For a warp, threads 0-31 access: base+0, base+1, ..., base+31 (contiguous), then base+D2+0, base+D2+1, ..., base+D2+31 (contiguous), etc. - This is fully coalesced! Good. But can we do better? Let me think about occupancy and instruction throughput. Current kernel: 256 threads per block, each does 4096/8 = 512 iterations of the unrolled loop, plus a few more. Each iteration does 8 loads + 8 fminf. For D1=4096, the unrolled loop runs 4096/8 = 512 times. For D1 not divisible by 8, we handle remainder. Wait, 4096 / 8 = 512 exactly. So no remainder loop. Each thread does 512 * 8 = 4096 loads and 4096 fminf operations. Total threads: B * ceil(D2/256) = 128 * 16 = 2048 blocks * 256 threads = 524,288 threads. Each thread accesses 4096 floats = 16KB of memory per thread. Total reads: 128 * 4096 * 4095 * 4 bytes ≈ 8.5 GB. Wait, that's only read bandwidth. The compute is just fminf, which is very light. Actually, let me think about whether we can vectorize loads. Using float4 or float2 to load 2 or 4 contiguous floats. But in this case, each thread only loads one float per d1 step. So we can't vectorize across d2 for a single thread because we only need one d2 per thread. But we could have each thread handle multiple d2 values to amortize the loop overhead and potentially vectorize. Let's try having each thread handle 4 d2 values (coalesced loads). Wait, actually let me try a different approach. Since we're reducing over D1=4096 and D2=4095, what if we transpose, reduce over the contiguous dim, then transpose back? Actually, [B, D1, D2] with D1 >> D2 (4096 vs 4095). Almost square. But reducing over D1 with stride D2=4095 is still coalesced if we do it right. Let me think about a better kernel design. What about a tree-based reduction using shared memory? For dim1 reduction: - Each block handles a tile of d2 values. Say 256 d2 values, 256 threads. - Each thread loads one d2 and then reduces over d1 iteratively. - But we can use shared memory to facilitate cooperation. Actually, another idea: What if each warp collaboratively reduces over D1 for a small tile of d2 values? Thread 255 handles 3072, 3073, 3074, 3075. But D2=4095, so valid. Block 4 (index 4) handles d2_base = 4096. That's beyond D2=4095. So block 4 should return early. Grid size for dim1 would be (D2 + 1023) / 1024 = (4095 + 1023) / 1024 = 5118 / 1024 = 4 (integer division). So 4 blocks per B. For blockIdx.x = 3, d2_base = 3*1024 + threadIdx.x*4. Thread 255: 3072 + 1020 = 4092. Plus 3 = 4095 (index 4094 is max valid). So d2_base 4092, 4093, 4094, 4095. Only 4095 is OOB. We handle this with the if check. Let me also consider whether we need more work per thread for dim0. Currently each thread handles 8 d2 values across B=128 iterations. That's 1024 loads. Maybe that's enough. For dim2, each warp handles 16 outputs and each lane reads ~4095/32 ≈ 128 values. That's also decent. Let me go ahead and implement. Actually, one more idea to explore: For dim1, what if I use 128 threads per block instead of 256, and have each thread handle 8 d2 values? 128 threads * 8 d2 = 1024 d2 per block (same). But fewer threads means fewer registers used, potentially allowing more blocks per SM. With 128 threads and ~24 registers each: 128 * 24 = 3072 registers per block. An SM has 65536 registers, so up to 21 blocks per SM (but limited to 32 total). So occupancy isn't an issue either way. But fewer threads means fewer warps = less ability to hide latency via thread switching. 128 threads = 4 warps. At 4 warps per SM with memory-bound kernel, we might not hide all latency. With 256 threads = 8 warps, we have more scheduling flexibility. So 256 threads is better. OK, let me finalize the kernel. I'll optimize dim1 with 4 d2 per thread and 16-unroll. For dim2 and dim0, I'll keep similar to current but clean up. Actually wait, let me reconsider whether handling 4 d2 values per thread is really better than 2. More work per thread means fewer total blocks. With B=128 and 4 blocks per B = 512 blocks. On H100 with 132 SMs, 512 blocks gives about 4 blocks per SM initially - but after they finish, wave after wave. Total waves = 512/132 ≈ 4 waves. That seems reasonable. With 2 d2 per thread: 8 blocks per B = 1024 blocks. 1024/132 ≈ 8 waves. More waves means more scheduling and completion overhead. But also more parallelism for latency hiding. With 1 d2 per thread: 16 blocks per B = 2048 blocks. 2048/132 ≈ 16 waves. Generally, fewer waves with more work per thread is better for throughput-oriented workloads. So 4 d2 per thread should be better. Let me also try to optimize dim2 similarly. Instead of 128 d1 per block with 256 threads, maybe 64 d1 per block with 128 threads or similar. Actually, for dim2, each output is a reduction over 4095 contiguous elements. A warp can easily reduce 4095 elements with warp shuffle. But we need to handle 128 outputs per block. In the current dim2 kernel, we use shared memory to stage elements, then warp shuffle. This adds overhead. What if each thread directly reduces its own subset? For dim2, if each block handles 64 outputs and each thread handles 8 outputs: - Block: 128 threads, each thread handles 8 d1 outputs. - Each thread reduces D2=4095 contiguous floats per output. - That's 8 * 4095 ≈ 32,768 loads per thread. A lot, but with ILP it might work. Wait, 128 threads * 64 outputs = 8192 outputs per block. For D1=4096, that's 4096/64 = 64 blocks per B. 64*128 = 8192 blocks total. Each block would read 64 * 4095 floats. Hmm, that's 32,768 loads per thread. If unrolled, the loop would be very long. Let me not go there. Alternative for dim2: each warp handles 1 output, uses warp shuffle to reduce. - 32 threads per warp reduce 4095 elements. - Each thread loads ~128 elements, maintains running min, then warp shuffle. - A block of 8 warps handles 8 outputs. - Grid: (4096/8 = 512, 128) = 65,536 blocks. Wait, that's too many blocks. Hmm. Let me compute: - 8 outputs per block, 128 threads. - D1=4096, so 4096/8 = 512 blocks in x dimension. - B=128, so 512 * 128 = 65,536 blocks. - With 132 SMs, that's 496 blocks per SM. Wait, no - at any given time only a few blocks fit per SM. Total waves = 65,536 / 132 ≈ 496 waves. That's a LOT of waves. Actually each block only does 8 * 4095 loads ≈ 32K loads. Total work is 65,536 blocks * 32K loads = 2B loads = 8 GB. But with so many tiny blocks, the kernel launch and scheduling overhead might be significant. So for dim2, having larger blocks is better. My current kernel of 128 outputs per block gives only 32 blocks in x dimension per B, 4096 blocks total. 4096/132 ≈ 31 waves. Much better. Let me keep the dim2 kernel as is, but maybe optimize it a bit. OK, I think I'll write the optimized dim1 kernel and keep decent versions for dim0 and dim2.

Wait, let me recalculate: 127 * 4096 * 4095 + 4095 * 4095 + 4095 = 127 * 4096 * 4095 + 4095 * (4095 + 1) = 127 * 4096 * 4095 + 4095 * 4096 = 4096 * 4095 * (127 + 1) = 4096 * 4095 * 128 = 2,147,450,880 This exactly equals the total elements. So index 2,147,450,879 is the last valid element, and 2,147,450,880 is one past the end. But memory allocations are usually padded to align to 256 bytes or similar. So reading one element past the end might not crash. Indeed, PyTorch tensors often have a bit of padding at the end. That's why correctness passed — the read was technically out of bounds but within the allocated memory block. Wait, the memory access pattern: for warp thread i accessing `base[i]` at d1=0, this is `input[b][0][d2+i]`. These are contiguous in memory. import torch
import os
os.environ['TORCH_CUDA_ARCH_LIST'] = '9.0'

from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = r"""
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <float.h>

__global__ void min_reduce_dim2(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out,
                                int B, int D1, int D2) {
    int b = blockIdx.y;
    int d1_base = blockIdx.x * 128;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    int lane = tid & 31;
    int warp_id = tid >> 5;
    
    if (d1_base >= D1) return;
    
    __shared__ float shm[128 * 32];
    __shared__ float running_smem[8 * 16];
    
    const float* in_base = in + (int64_t)b * D1 * D2 + (int64_t)d1_base * D2;
    
    if (lane == 0) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = 0; i < 16; i++) {
            running_smem[warp_id * 16 + i] = FLT_MAX;
        }
    }
    
    int num_tiles = (D2 + 31) / 32;
    
    for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) {
        int d2_offset = tile * 32;
        int load_width = min(32, D2 - d2_offset);
        
        for (int s = 0; s < 16; s++) {
            int idx = s * 256 + tid;
            int o = idx >> 5;
            int e = idx & 31;
            int d1 = d1_base + o;
            if (d1 < D1) {
                shm[o * 32 + e] = (e < load_width) ? in_base[(int64_t)o * D2 + d2_offset + e] : FLT_MAX;
            }
        }
        __syncthreads();
        
        int warp_output_start = warp_id * 16;
        #pragma unroll
        for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) {
            int out_idx = warp_output_start + o;
            if (d1_base + out_idx < D1) {
                float val = shm[out_idx * 32 + lane];
                if (lane >= load_width) val = FLT_MAX;
                #pragma unroll
                for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
                    float other = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset);
                    val = fminf(val, other);
                }
                if (lane == 0) {
                    running_smem[warp_id * 16 + o] = fminf(running_smem[warp_id * 16 + o], val);
                }
            }
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    
    if (lane == 0) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int o = 0; o < 16; o++) {
            int d1 = d1_base + warp_id * 16 + o;
            if (d1 < D1) {
                out[(int64_t)b * D1 + d1] = running_smem[warp_id * 16 + o];
            }
        }
    }
}

__global__ void min_reduce_dim1(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out,
                                int B, int D1, int D2) {
    int b = blockIdx.y;
    int d2 = blockIdx.x * 256 + threadIdx.x;
    if (d2 >= D2) return;
    
    float m0 = FLT_MAX, m1 = FLT_MAX, m2 = FLT_MAX, m3 = FLT_MAX;
    float m4 = FLT_MAX, m5 = FLT_MAX, m6 = FLT_MAX, m7 = FLT_MAX;
    
    const float* base = in + (int64_t)b * D1 * D2 + d2;
    const int stride = D2;
    
    int d1 = 0;
    for (; d1 + 7 < D1; d1 += 8) {
        m0 = fminf(m0, base[0]);
        m1 = fminf(m1, base[stride]);
        m2 = fminf(m2, base[2*stride]);
        m3 = fminf(m3, base[3*stride]);
        m4 = fminf(m4, base[4*stride]);
        m5 = fminf(m5, base[5*stride]);
        m6 = fminf(m6, base[6*stride]);
        m7 = fminf(m7, base[7*stride]);
        base += 8 * stride;
    }
    
    for (; d1 < D1; d1++) {
        m0 = fminf(m0, base[0]);
        base += stride;
    }
    
    m0 = fminf(m0, m1);
    m2 = fminf(m2, m3);
    m4 = fminf(m4, m5);
    m6 = fminf(m6, m7);
    
    m0 = fminf(m0, m2);
    m4 = fminf(m4, m6);
    
    m0 = fminf(m0, m4);
    
    out[(int64_t)b * D2 + d2] = m0;
}

__global__ void min_reduce_dim0(const float* __restrict__ in, float* __restrict__ out,
                                int B, int D1, int D2) {
    int d2_base = blockIdx.x * 256;
    int d1_base = blockIdx.y * 8;
    int lane = threadIdx.x & 31;
    int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5;
    int d1 = d1_base + warp_id;
    int d2 = d2_base + lane;
    
    if (d1 >= D1 || d2 >= D2) return;
    
    float mins[8];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) mins[i] = FLT_MAX;
    
    for (int b = 0; b < B; b++) {
        const float* ptr = in + (int64_t)b * D1 * D2 + (int64_t)d1 * D2 + d2;
        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {
            if (d2 + i * 32 < D2) {
                mins[i] = fminf(mins[i], ptr[i * 32]);
            }
        }
    }
    
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {
        if (d2 + i * 32 < D2) {
            out[(int64_t)d1 * D2 + d2 + i * 32] = mins[i];
        }
    }
}

torch::Tensor min_reduce_forward(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim) {
    int ndim = input.dim();
    if (dim < 0) dim += ndim;
    
    auto sizes = input.sizes();
    std::vector<int64_t> out_sizes;
    for (int i = 0; i < ndim; i++) {
        if (i != dim) out_sizes.push_back(sizes[i]);
    }
    auto output = torch::empty(out_sizes, input.options());
    
    if (ndim == 3 && input.is_cuda() && input.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32) {
        int B = sizes[0];
        int D1 = sizes[1];
        int D2 = sizes[2];
        
        if (dim == 2) {
            dim3 block(256);
            dim3 grid((D1 + 127) / 128, B);
            min_reduce_dim2<<<grid, block>>>(
                input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, D1, D2);
        } else if (dim == 1) {
            dim3 block(256);
            dim3 grid((D2 + 255) / 256, B);
            min_reduce_dim1<<<grid, block>>>(
                input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, D1, D2);
        } else if (dim == 0) {
            dim3 block(256);
            dim3 grid((D2 + 255) / 256, (D1 + 7) / 8);
            min_reduce_dim0<<<grid, block>>>(
                input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, D1, D2);
        }
    }
    return output;
}
"""

min_reduce = load_inline(
    name="min_reduce_submit",
    cpp_sources="torch::Tensor min_reduce_forward(torch::Tensor input, int64_t dim);",
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["min_reduce_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    extra_ldflags=["-static-libstdc++", "-static-libgcc"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, dim: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return min_reduce.min_reduce_forward(x, self.dim)
